scope="row" class="infobox-label">Website</th><td class="infobox-data"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.doradoparaiso.com">www<wbr />.doradoparaiso<wbr />.com</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Dorado</b> (<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1177148991">.mw-parser-output .IPA-label-small{font-size:85%}.mw-parser-output .references .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .infobox .IPA-label-small,.mw-parser-output .navbox .IPA-label-small{font-size:100%}</style><span class="IPA-label IPA-label-small">Spanish pronunciation:</span> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="es-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">&#91;do\xcb\x88\xc9\xbea\xc3\xb0o&#93;</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Dorado_barrio-pueblo" title="Dorado barrio-pueblo">town</a> and <a href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Municipalities of Puerto Rico">municipality</a> on the northern coast of <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">Puerto Rico</a>, 15 miles (24&#160;km) west of <a href="/wiki/San_Juan,_Puerto_Rico" title="San Juan, Puerto Rico">San Juan</a> and is located in the northern region of the island, bordering the Atlantic Ocean, north of <a href="/wiki/Toa_Alta,_Puerto_Rico" title="Toa Alta, Puerto Rico">Toa Alta</a>, east of <a href="/wiki/Vega_Alta,_Puerto_Rico" title="Vega Alta, Puerto Rico">Vega Alta</a>, and west of <a href="/wiki/Toa_Baja,_Puerto_Rico" title="Toa Baja, Puerto Rico">Toa Baja</a>. Dorado is subdivided into five <a href="/wiki/Barrio" title="Barrio">barrios</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dorado_barrio-pueblo" title="Dorado barrio-pueblo">Dorado Pueblo</a> (the downtown area and the administrative center of the city). It is part of the <a href="/wiki/San_Juan-Caguas-Guaynabo_Metropolitan_Statistical_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="San Juan-Caguas-Guaynabo Metropolitan Statistical Area">San Juan-Caguas-Guaynabo Metropolitan Statistical Area</a>. During the early 18th century, there were already mentions of a <i>"Sitio de Dorado"</i> (meaning a golden place) in some San Juan registers.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since the beginning of the Spanish colonial period and until 1831, <a href="/wiki/Dorado_barrio-pueblo" title="Dorado barrio-pueblo">Dorado</a> existed as a barrio (or <a href="/wiki/Ward_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ward (politics)">ward</a>) of the town of <a href="/wiki/Toa_Baja" class="mw-redirect" title="Toa Baja">Toa Baja</a>. Over several years, the ward grew and established its own town center called the "new pueblo" to differentiate itself from Toa Baja, which became known as the "old pueblo." Over several years, the barrios that currently make up Dorado grew and the people of the "new pueblo" wanted to separate themselves from Toa Baja.\n</p><p>On November 22, 1842, Jacinto L\xc3\xb3pez Mart\xc3\xadnez, the Sergeant at Arms for the ward of Dorado, petitioned the Spanish <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Puerto_Rico" title="Governor of Puerto Rico">Governor of Puerto Rico</a>, Santiago M\xc3\xa9ndez Vigo, to establish the municipality of Dorado. The governor authorized the founding of the town pending the construction of public works, including an administrative building and a church near the town square. In 1848, the construction of the public works were completed and L\xc3\xb3pez Mart\xc3\xadnez became the first mayor of Dorado. Puerto Rico was ceded by Spain in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War">Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War</a> under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_of_1898" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Paris of 1898">Treaty of Paris of 1898</a> and became a territory of the United States. In 1902, four years after the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War#Puerto_Rico" title="Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War">Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War</a>, Dorado was again appended to Toa Baja. But in 1905 it regained its status as a separate town. Nowadays, Dorado has upscale neighborhoods and a small downtown area with a <i>plaza</i> (main <a href="/wiki/Town_square" title="Town square">town square</a>), as other Puerto Rican municipalities. The town\'s patron saint is <a href="/wiki/Saint_Anthony_of_Padua" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Anthony of Padua">Anthony of Padua</a>, and patron saint celebrations are held at the plaza every year on June 13.\n</p>\n<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado,_Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Pre-Columbian_period_and_colonization">Pre-Columbian period and colonization</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado,_Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Pre-Columbian period and colonization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>The region adjacent Toa river (modern day La Plata river) was inhabited by several indigenous groups that left behind physical proof of their presence in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino22_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino22-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The area surrounding the Toa river was scouted by <a href="/wiki/Juan_Ponce_de_Le%C3%B3n" title="Juan Ponce de Le\xc3\xb3n">Juan Ponce de Le\xc3\xb3n</a> during his initial reconnaissance of the geography of the main island.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino21_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino21-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Near it, the Conquistadores built some huts in which to rest.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino21_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino21-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, citing "difficulties" with the region, the Spaniards opted to move to the region that would eventually become Caparra, the colonial Spanish capital of San Juan Bautista (the original Spanish name for Puerto Rico).<sup id="cite_ref-Canino22_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino22-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the beginning of the Spanish colonization, the area known as Toa was ruled by Ta\xc3\xadno cacique (a title akin to Chief) Araman\xc3\xa1, who controlled the regions comprising Punta Salinas to Cibuco river (modern day Toa Baja through Vega Baja).<sup id="cite_ref-Canino22_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino22-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After Ag\xc3\xbceybana I (a title akin to High Chief or king) entered in association with the Spanish conquistadors through the guaytiao he became known as Francisco de Araman\xc3\xa1, also being recognized as a <a href="/wiki/Don_(honorific)" title="Don (honorific)">Don</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino22_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino22-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Now technically as politically influential as Ag\xc3\xbceybana due to the ceremony, the Spanish were able to include the lower ranking caciques, including Araman\xc3\xa1, in the <i>encomiendas</i> system and placed several in charge of royal farms and mining operations near Toa river.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino22_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino22-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The region proved production both in agriculture and mining and an assimilation process began between both cultures.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino24_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino24-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ponce de Le\xc3\xb3n himself created Hacienda del Rey and requested the creation of a new conuco near Toa river, which was later sold to Pedro Ortiz.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino24_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino24-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The conquistador also built other properties that he later sold to other Spaniards.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino25_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino25-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Around 500 Ta\xc3\xadnos worked under the caciques in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino23_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino23-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Oral tradition claims that a Spaniard named Juan Gonz\xc3\xa1lez somehow survived long enough after being severely injured in the initial Ta\xc3\xadno offensive of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93Ta%C3%ADno_War_of_San_Juan%E2%80%93Borik%C3%A9n" title="Spanish\xe2\x80\x93Ta\xc3\xadno War of San Juan\xe2\x80\x93Borik\xc3\xa9n">Spanish\xe2\x80\x93Ta\xc3\xadno War of San Juan\xe2\x80\x93Borik\xc3\xa9n</a> to reach the region of Toa and warn the conquistadores.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino26_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino26-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ten years after the beginning if the colonization, more than 17 estates were built in the region, with harvests being transported through the Toa river to Palo Seco and to the Capital from there.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino26_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino26-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After Ponce de Le\xc3\xb3n transferred Hacienda del Rey to other Spaniards, the estate steadily declined and by 1524 denounces were made about its state.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino25_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino25-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As more Spanish families settled, problems between neighbors arose, prompting Francisco de Negrete to request that a mayor was assigned to the region in 1611.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino28_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino28-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, it is not until 1731 that ra mayor, Juan D\xc3\xa1vila, appears in the records.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino28_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino28-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the following decades, more than 24 mayors would serve office, with military officers taking prominence beginning in the 1740s.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino29_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino29-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Existence_as_part_of_Toa_Baja">Existence as part of Toa Baja</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado,_Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Existence as part of Toa Baja"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>On September 11, 1627, San Juan\'s council ordered the creation of a ship route between Bayam\xc3\xb3n and Toa, giving control of the rent to the council of the first city.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino17_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino17-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> From here, the rest of the route was traversed on horseback.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino17_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino17-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On the way from the Capital to the central north coast, two passages were encountered, the Palo Seco and the Boca Hababa river.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino17_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino17-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After crossing the second, a rest area was built in modern-day Barrio Mameyal.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino18_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino18-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With an increase in traffic, the inhabitants of the town of Toa saw business opportunities and began joining the local fishermen, steadily creating a small settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino18_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino18-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By the first half of the 18th century, the locale was already known as "Dorado", "Boca del Dorado" or "Alto del Dorado" and referred to as such in official documentation.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino18_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino18-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The municipality of Toa Baja was founded either in 1745.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino29_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino29-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other sources place the foundation taking place in 1747. With this development, the region became part of it.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino19_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino19-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Santa_Hermandad" title="Santa Hermandad">Santa Hermandad</a> mayor would then become municipal mayor.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino29_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino29-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1748, the Settler Captain was George Garc\xc3\xada P\xc3\xa1es.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino30_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino30-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In February 1761, there was a conflict between the overseer of the Boca Havana passage, Cayetano Qui\xc3\xb1ones, and the local fishermen in which the location became relevant.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino18_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino18-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The control and lent of these passages remained in contention during the following century, with the San Juan council refereeing similar issues.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino19_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino19-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Towards the 18th century\'s end and entering the 19th century, the municipality had become a hub for sugar plantations led by Spanish hacendados and slavers, who exploited an international need for sugar caused by the Haitian revolution to export it and derivatives like rum while also meeting local demands.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino31_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino31-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other crops were also grown, but no priority was given to them.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino31_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino31-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The end of the Napoleonic wars and the elimination of some taxes allowed a new generation of hacendados from the capital of San Juan to benefit from the booming industry, among the mayors Jacinto L\xc3\xb3pez Mart\xc3\xadnez and Jos\xc3\xa9 Canales.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino33_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino33-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the roads that lead to Western Puerto Rico crossed through el Dorado and this, combined with flooding concerns, did not allow the municipality to develop a proper urban center, with Palo Seco being its main commerce epicenter.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino33_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino33-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1820, Dorado itself was as big as the municipal town and the crisis lead to a proposal of relocating the municipal town there being presented to colonial governor Miguel de la Torre by <i>hacendados</i> with ties to him during a visit.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino34_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino34-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On October 20, 1824, the move was authorized, but it stalled due to opposition from the settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino35_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino35-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, this same year a hurricane named Santa Ana ravaged and flooded the municipal town, causing the death of people and cattle, accelerating the process.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino36_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino36-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following years, the construction of a royal house and a church had begun at Dorado.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino35_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino35-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During this time, Toa Baja\'s mayor Jos\xc3\xa9 Mar\xc3\xad Ram\xc3\xadrez de Arellano requested moving the boat access point near the entry of Dorado to a more accessible location, avoiding dangerous body of waters in the process, which was later conceded by the council of San Juan.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino37_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino37-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the free transit to Dorado for the neighbors of the municipal town of Toa Baja was attended afterwards, despite being part of the initial request.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino39_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino39-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1829, the local militias and the justice offices were relocated as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino36_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino36-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, entering the following decades the dissidents of the move led by mayor Jos\xc3\xa9 Manuel de C\xc3\xb3rdova extra officially forced the return of the municipal institutions and returned them to Toa Baja, claiming lack of proper maintenance.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino39_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino39-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite this, Dorado continued growing as the traffic of people traversing through the royal road rose.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino40_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino40-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1832, a teacher named Jos\xc3\xa9 Viada was teaching the local children.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino135_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino135-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The settlement eventually earned the nickname of "Pueblo Nuevo" to differentiate it from Toa Baja ("Pueblo Viejo").<sup id="cite_ref-Canino331_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino331-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Secession_disputes_with_Toa_Baja">Secession disputes with Toa Baja</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado,_Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Secession disputes with Toa Baja"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>With the plantations led by administrators, the flourish in the sugar industry continued until 1840, when the market entered in a crisis and the price if products crashed, leaving the region with a declining industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino33_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino33-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1839, Palo Seco opted to become a separate municipality.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino34_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino34-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On March 10, 1841, colonial governor Santiago de M\xc3\xa9ndez Vigo authorized moving all the administrative offices from Dorado to Toa Baja, faced by pressure from the settlers.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino39_21-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino39-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Consequently, former mayor and hacendado Jacinto L\xc3\xb3pez Mart\xc3\xadnez, who had employed his friendship with De la Torre to acquire local terrains between 1813 and 1835, proposed creating a separate municipality by the name of El Dorado to secure his wealth by acquiring the title of Settlement Captain.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino40_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino40-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In a reunion with friends and family, he formalized this request.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino43_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino43-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The justification was based in the population growth in Dorado and adjacent barrios and possessing enough resources to sustain itself separately.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino44_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino44-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In arrangements that took place on July 27, 1841, in San Juan, L\xc3\xb3pez was joined by Jos\xc3\xa9 de Folgueras as prospective beneficiaries if the move and his proposal was supported by Manuel Canales, Celedonio Nev\xc3\xa1rez, Juan P. Nev\xc3\xa1rez, Domingo L\xc3\xb3pez, Andr\xc3\xa9s Mart\xc3\xadnez, Florencio S\xc3\xa1nchez, Jos\xc3\xa9 M. L\xc3\xb3pez, Ignacio Arraza\xc3\xadn and Ignacio Cordero, who offered to build the buildings necessary to sustain the town.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino43_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino43-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The official request took place shortly afterwards, with the governor requesting feedback from the municipality of Toa Baja after being counseled to do so.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino44_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino44-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After more than a month, the reply contained strong opposition to the separation, heavily criticizing the capacities of those involved in the request citing personal ambitions as a motive and claiming that the lands were unproductive, that the area was plagued with environmental factors that "resulted in the early death" of its residents and that the water was unclean.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino44_26-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino44-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The rebuttal also claimed that the barrios pushing for separation were unable too poor to sustain the public workings of a town, despite being the ones that contributed most to the fisco up to this point, the purported lack of personnel and a workforce or that the proximity of the settlements would be unlawful.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino47_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino47-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After being given copies of Toa Baja\'s opposition, L\xc3\xb3pez and Folgueras requested that the complaints were dismissed and questioned the accuracy of any of the counterarguments, also opting to criticize that some of the proponents were being targeted for being poor, which should not prevent them from entering association.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino49_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino49-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The group also labelled Toa Baja as a failed municipality by using its iwn criteria, responding that the criticism for lack of a workforce was unfair since despite its age the town faced similar problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino50_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino50-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Public hearings were then proposed and accepted by the colonial governor.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino50_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino50-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The process was assigned to Commander Miguel Delgado, who left to Toa Baja accompanied by witnesses Pl\xc3\xa1cido de Cabrera and Juan M. Feijo\xc3\xb3.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino50_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino50-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following day, March 10, 1842, the preliminary preparations began.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino50_29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino50-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Only 14 of the 120 available testified in the hearings, which were interviewed about their residence in Dorado and expressed favor for the creation of a municipality.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino51_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino51-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>a<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Delgado also supervised the status of progression in the construction of a Catholic church.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino51_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino51-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On March 13, 1842, a process to interview the opponents at Toa Baja, who had since stepped our from authority, began and extended for three days.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino52_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino52-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Afterwards, Delgado returned to San Juan with a report for the governor favoring the establishment of a new municipality due to favorable conditions and military potential and discarding proximity as a factor, since Camuy and Hatillo were already proximate.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino52_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino52-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> M\xc3\xa9ndez Vigo delegated the issue to an aid, Anacleto Buelta, who suggested interviewing the current Toa Baja administration and argued inconsistencies in witness selection.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino52_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino52-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Delgado died suddenly shortly afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino53_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino53-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Buelta then recommended beginning a new process again in Toa Baja, which the colonial governor conceded and assigned the task to councilor Domingo Garc\xc3\xada.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino53_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino53-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On May 28, 1842, he left the capital accompanied by witnesses Pl\xc3\xa1cido de Cabrera and Eusebio de Cabrera.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino53_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino53-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The second set of hearings began on June 1, 1842, in the town hall of Toa Baja, which assigned a commission to oversee the process, with several of the previous deponents ratifying their previous statements and 23 inhabitants of Dorado supporting the request for separation, with the remainder being forced to attend subsequent hearings by the local court.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino55_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino55-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> All ratified their previous support except five, who felt that they would be forced to work in the church, a job that they had left at Toa Baja.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino57_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino57-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The only exceptions, according to the rural mayors of Dorado and Maguayo, were either sick or absent.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Toa Baja Commission, represented by mayor Juan Landr\xc3\xb3n an overseer and the municipal secretary, proposed that only proprietors, those that paid subsidies, should be consulted, considering the declarations of those that didn\'t useless and potentially damaging to those that did.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino58_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino58-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The municipal government also opposed the declarations of no -resident proponents, those that weren\'t family heads or that had any connection with the main proponents, accompanied by a list of people that they considered ineligible.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino60_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino60-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Founding_of_Dorado">Founding of Dorado</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado,_Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Founding of Dorado"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>On June 4, 1842, Landr\xc3\xb3n submitted a list of people that were considered eligible by his administration, including residents from barrios that were not initially interested in the action.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino61_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino61-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This same day, opposition hearings were held where the administration if Toa Baja presented its case, with L\xc3\xb3pez and Folgueras serving as witnesses.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino62_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino62-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A total of 91 neighbors from these barrios attended, with the opinions regarding the foundation of Dorado being in favor of the opposition 125\xe2\x80\x938, without counting a number of indifferent attendees.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino62_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino62-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hearing concluded on June 9, 1842, with a relevant report being completed the same day.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino64_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino64-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Like the first, it was delegated to an aide.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino64_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino64-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two days later, L\xc3\xb3pez addressed the colonial governor requesting a copy of the aide\'s conclusion, citing the suspicion that the municipality of Toa Baja had used ineligible witnesses to dilate the process.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino64_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino64-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Shortly afterwards, L\xc3\xb3pez impugned the declarations of opposing witnesses, noting that most did not live near the proposed municipality and that others simply wanted to avoid paying more to the government, requesting statistics of paying inhabitants of Dorado with which to argue that they were capable of sustaining a new town.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino65_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino65-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On August 12, 1842, the proponents sent a recount of the process and requested that the colonial governor authorized the founding of Dorado, printing the aid to request the opinion of the Toa Baja administration.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino65_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino65-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On September 8, 1842, the municipal government once again opposed the separation.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino66_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino66-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After receiving it, aide Melit\xc3\xb3n Belanzathegui recommended proceeding with the foundation of a new municipality, with M\xc3\xa9ndez Vigo authorizing the creation of San Antonio del Dorado on November 22, 1842.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino66_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino66-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The government made demands guaranteeing the construction of several building, towards which L\xc3\xb3pez placed his own properties as collateral.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino67_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino67-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The founders also requested to begin the process to assign or elect relevant authority figures, all of which were granted.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino68_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino68-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The colonial governor also named L\xc3\xb3pez Settler Captain and ordered that all buildings were completed within a year and a half.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino68_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino68-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The demarcation process was initially overseen by Pedro Garc\xc3\xada with the help of Julio O\'Neill of the Bayam\xc3\xb3n Department with Carlos Vuagneaux taking charge following a suspenci\xc3\xb3n due to weather, and began on January 10, 1843, extending for six days.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino68_45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino68-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The cost of establishing the new municipality ascended 4,196 pesos with 20 cents.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino69_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino69-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite the founding of a number of schools, the education in the new municipality was troubled by the instability in the permanency of teachers appointed by the Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino135_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino135-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>During this time, there were around 17 high class residences in the municipality, while the working class lived in wood huts.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino185_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino185-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Depending on an agricultural economy, there were several hundred African slaves working in the Spanish haciendas of Dorado.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino88_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino88-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On March 6, 1843, there was a slave uprising in the hacienda of Francisco Cantero, who after being received by gunfire in the Catholic Church in Toa Baja, fled to the sugar plantations of Dorado, where the government captured them and sentenced several to death.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino87_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino87-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite royal decrees prohibiting the traffic of African slaves, the Spaniards continued illegally importing them, with Dorado mayor Jos\xc3\xa9 Carreras actively participating in the trade.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino88_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino88-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the salve population was severely hit by a cholera epidemic, and two years later the colonial government confiscated a ship property of Carreras was confiscated due to its suspected links with the activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino91_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino91-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Consternation in the colonial authorities lead to the slaves being permitted to file complaints before the Municipal Sindico Receiver.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino98_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino98-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A predominantly Catholic culture also brought another phenomenon, in which repentant slave owners set them free as part if their last will.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino100_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino100-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other work sources, like corral fishing, required permission first from the council of San Juan and later from the municipal administration.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino133_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino133-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1848, the municipality of Dorado was running all of its functions under L\xc3\xb3pez, now mayor and War Lieutenant and in charge of filling several reports related to the population, wealth and agricultural production.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino71_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino71-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Afterwards, the mayor would be elected by the governor from a pool of three candidates selected by the municipality\'s elite and only served a maximum of two years.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino76_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino76-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During this time, the economy was dependent on 9 sugar haciendas and 54 cattle estates.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino103_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino103-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The industry in question was dominated by L\xc3\xb3pez himself, Manuel Skerrett, Francisco Cantero, Jos\xc3\xa9 Marrero, Carlos Vassallo, Miguel Torrens, Juan P. Nev\xc3\xa1rez, Florencio Salgado, and the inheritors of Mar\xc3\xada E. Mart\xc3\xadnez.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino104_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino104-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Being located in the Camino Real (Royal Highway) grocery and other types of stores were run by people like Antonio Sol\xc3\xa9, Benito Carreras, and Juana S\xc3\xa1nchez and brothers.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino121_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino121-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These types of businesses continued flourishing for the following decades.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino125_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino125-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1859, there were 179 farms in Dorado.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino106_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino106-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> L\xc3\xb3pez, Salgado and others also created brick and lime powder factories.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino128_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino128-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At the First Public Exposition of Industry, Agriculture and Arts, Lechet demonstrated his advances in processing white sugar, which earned him a silver commendation from the Secretary of the Royal Commerce Board, Andr\xc3\xa9s Vi\xc3\xb1a.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino129_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino129-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the third edition of this event, a Dorado factory won the gold award for a variety if products.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino129_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino129-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Abolition_of_slavery_and_Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American_War"><span id="Abolition_of_slavery_and_Spanish.E2.80.93American_War"></span>Abolition of slavery and Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado,_Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Abolition of slavery and Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>On March 19, 1850, the municipality was reclassified as a Second Class Mayorship.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino71_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino71-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On August 4, 1860, Toa Baja mayor Manuel Aguayo proposed the merger of Dorado into Toa Baja, which was opposed by Dirado mayor Pablo A. Due\xc3\xb1o and his administration.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino75_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino75-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A month later the proposal was dropped by the colonial governor.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino75_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino75-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Upon his death in 1862, L\xc3\xb3pez himself left money for his slaves.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino101_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino101-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His inheritors failed to preserve the terrains that were received from him.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino106_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino106-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1870, a group of four slaves -named Manuel D\xc3\xadaz, Juan Pedro, Juan D. Epifanio and Benito- killed the butler of hacienda La Monserrate, Manuel M. Sampayo Salda\xc3\xb1a, who had developed a reputation for the eating the violently.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino95_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino95-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For this act, the colonial government executed them on September 26, 1870.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino95_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino95-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The event would become part of Dorado\'s oral tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino95_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino95-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Despite drop in the local price of slaves, some residents of Cuba would buy slaves and export them to that island where the price was rising.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino96_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino96-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1872, an incident involving a Frenchman named Jos\xc3\xa9 Beaupied lead to the slaver being intervened and losing the four children of a slave that he had bought, but she was transferred to Dorado and with the help of a fellow Frenchman and later committed suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino98_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino98-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The event was controversial and lead to the local British ambassador, H. August Cowper, protesting the moving if slaves between the islands before the Spanish crown.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino98_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino98-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Telegraph lines were placed in 1870, connecting Dirado to several municipalities and remaining in service for more than a century.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino190_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino190-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>As abolition became impending, several entities including Sucesi\xc3\xb3n de Torrens, Sobrinos de Arraza\xc3\xadn and Skerret y Hermanos joined individuals such as Ambrosio Martorell and released their slaves pro bono.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino101_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino101-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For thus action, they were commended by the Spanish Crown on April 26, 1873.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino101_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino101-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In total, the abolition granted freedom to 402 slaves in Dorado.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino102_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino102-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Oral tradition claims that the freed celebrated until midnight, when a thunderstorm suddenly appeared and during which the tomb of Sampayo was struck by lightning.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino102_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino102-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A series of earthquakes in late 1867 damaged buildings in Dorado, particularly the Catholic Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino187_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino187-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1872, local instruction was placed in charge of Jos\xc3\xa9 Iglesias and six years later the situation began improving.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino135_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino135-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1884, Jos\xc3\xa9 D. F\xc3\xa9lix and Pedro M. Ag\xc3\xbceeo founded private schools, which attended an interest of the higher classes to educate their children.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino137_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino137-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Entering the next decade, there were six public schools and several hundred children being educated.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino137_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino137-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the elementary system was troubled, with only two schools classified as Second Class by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino138_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino138-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By this time there were more than 200 houses in the urban area of Dorado and over 270 families.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino185_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino185-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Dorado remained a poor municipality throughout the 19th century and the 1873 agricultural crisis exacerbated its issues to the point that towards 1897 was unable to pay debts that had been accumulating for nearly 30 years.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino72_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino72-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During this crisis, several of the hacendados were forced to mortgage their haciendas, incurring in large debts that lead to some losing them.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino74_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino74-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On July 20, 1870, the annexation to Toa Baja was proposed by conservatives, but failed to proceed.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino75_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino75-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Early during this decade, there were seven businesses that paid subsidies, while during the second half there were some 14 businesses.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino126_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino126-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On June 10, 1873, the municipality of Dorado requested permission to widen and deepen the La Plata river mouth to allow larger ships to enter in an attempt to reinvigorate the sugar industry.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino130_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino130-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, a prolonged draught seriously affected the crops and combined with the payment that was now given to former slaves, the situation quickly declined.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino130_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino130-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Jacinto L\xc3\xb3pez II became mayor in 1875, holding the office until the year of his death nine years later, but despite this influence debt made him lose the terrains that once belonged to his father to the colonial government.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino107_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino107-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The budgets were not satisfactory, but Dorado received the control of the passage of La Plata, which it rented.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino73_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino73-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the passage soon became a point of contention between those that wanted to oversee it and the municipality, including a failed 1878 litigation presented to the colonial governor himself during a visit.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino73_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino73-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By this year, there were only seven steam powered sugar mills working in the municipality.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino104_57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino104-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>On March 8, 1891, the train track connecting Dorado to Manat\xc3\xad and Mart\xc3\xadn Pe\xc3\xb1a were inaugurated.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino191_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino191-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The passenger train would operate until the 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino191_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino191-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A sales tax was imposed to try and counter the crisis in 1895, but within a year the abolition of Dorado as a municipality was considered.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino75_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino75-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The final decade of the century borough forth a sugar renaissance, growing to 11 haciendas which were complemented by a coffee estate.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino104_57-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino104-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1893, there were 229 farms, with seven totaling more than half of the terrains with Pablo Ubarri being the dominant owner.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino107_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino107-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As consequence of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War">Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War</a>, Dorado was placed in charge of the colonial American government on October 11, 1898.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino123_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino123-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="American_military_administration">American military administration</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado,_Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: American military administration"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>Puerto Rico was ceded by Spain in the aftermath of the <a href="/wiki/Spanish%E2%80%93American_War" title="Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War">Spanish\xe2\x80\x93American War</a> under the terms of the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Paris_of_1898" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Paris of 1898">Treaty of Paris of 1898</a> and became a territory of the United States. In 1899, the United States conducted its first census of Puerto Rico finding that the population of Dorado was 3,804.<sup id="cite_ref-OfficeSanger1900_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OfficeSanger1900-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>This change in sovereignty resulted in several of the local <a href="/wiki/Hacienda" title="Hacienda">haciendas</a> being systematically redistributed between Americans such as R.S. Brown, William W. Miner and corporations like Y.P.R. Fruit Land Co. and Finlay Waymouth and Lee, Inc. during the following decade.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino123_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino123-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ubarri\'s terrains were fragmented and part of it ended being owned by Afred Livingston and his daughter Clara.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino123_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino123-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Parraoquial House was seized by the colonial government and in 1922 the municipality turned it into an emergency building.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino187_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino187-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Elections for municipal offices began being held on January 16, 1900.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino81_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino81-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The results were certified by representative of the colonial government, Lt. Woodson Hocker.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino82_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino82-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1902, there were six schools in Dorado.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino138_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino138-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These institutions would later be staffed by Dorade\xc3\xb1os that graduated from the University of Puerto Rico, completed by other locals.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino139_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino139-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With time, some families would dedicate to instruction.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino141_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino141-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By the second decade of the century, Escuela Jacinto L\xc3\xb3pez Mart\xc3\xadnez and Segunda Unidad de Maguayo were built to accommodate the increasing school age population, with the first being created at the behest of mayor Heraclio L\xc3\xb3pez Canino.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino142_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino142-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Alfredo L\xc3\xb3pez would take the office of mayor from his father and begin a series of urban development projects.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino306_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino306-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following two decades, Pedro L\xc3\xb3pez Canino and two rural schools were built.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino142_85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino142-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Near it, the Escuela Ricardo Arroyo Laracuente served as middle school.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino143_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino143-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1952, Manuel Morales Garc\xc3\xada was elected as mayor.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino307_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino307-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1964, a local high school was built so that local children did not have to travel to Toa Baja, the following decade it was renamed after local attorney Jos\xc3\xa9 S. Alegr\xc3\xada.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino142_85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino142-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Pedro L\xc3\xb3pez Canino served as mayor taking over the municipal administration when Dorado\'s budget was merely $3,000, with this sum ascending to $18,000 by the 1920s.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino270_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino270-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first telephone lines were placed in 1910 and operators were employed until the 1960s.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino190_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino190-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> That same year, the Puerto Rico Power and Light Co. installed electrical lines.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino192_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino192-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>New stores were founded early in this century, including El Siglo XX and Salgado y P\xc3\xa9rez, both of which ceased to exist during the first half of the century.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino126_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino126-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first gas station in Dorado was founded by Ram\xc3\xb3n P\xc3\xa9rez, who also created the Dorada Bus Line.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino127_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino127-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Milk and ice were also distributed.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino128_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino128-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Maritime and fresh water fishers were responsible for providing fresh fish and several would later become part of Dorado\'s oral tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino133_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino133-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the 1950s, the Dorado Fishing Club introduced sport fishing to the municipality.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino134_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino134-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Ultimately, Dorado was annexed to Toa Alta in 1902, when the Puerto Rico legislature ordered the move due to its economic woes.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino76_55-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino76-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In March 1905, the same entity reversed the move.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino76_55-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino76-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1920, 177 farms remained, with a sharp decline in mid-sized and small properties.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino125_59-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino125-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Three years after the death of Alfred Livingston in 1923, the government reclaimed Mata Redonda farm since it had been acquired illegally, but after being repelled with armed resistance during the impending, the case was seen by the colonial Federal Court, which failed in favor of Clara Livingston.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino124_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino124-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With the foreign interests, cultivation of sugar rose and reached 400 square meters (a unit known in Spanish as "cuerda") of land, but coffee stopped being produced.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino125_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino125-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other fruits began increasing production during the 1940s.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino125_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino125-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most of the population was dependent on the opportunities offered by the agricultural entities, which lead to a pronounced unemployment rate.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino134_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino134-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To counter this, the worker\'s union donated the building where the Dorado Hand Craft factory was established, operating for two decades.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino134_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino134-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> New aqueducts and a sewer system replaced a well based system during 1948\xe2\x80\x9350.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino192_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino192-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1936, a new bridge crossing La Plata river was inaugurated.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino362_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino362-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1940s, there was widespread construction of public and private housing to accommodate a series of government reforms.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino193_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino193-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Among these was El Pueblo del Ni\xc3\xb1o, a refuge for children.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino193_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino193-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1947, the municipal assembly adopted the official map of Dorado that was proposed with the barrios of Higuillar, Maguayo, Espinosa, Mameyal and R\xc3\xado Lajas.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino362_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino362-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Commonwealth">Commonwealth</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado,_Puerto_Rico&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Commonwealth"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>\n<p>In 1953, a widespread fire destroyed 11 houses, with its residents being relocated to new homes provided by charity.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino193_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino193-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This precipitated the creation of a local fire station in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino194_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino194-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The end of the Korean War lead to another urbanization wave.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino195_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino195-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The construction of private project Urbanizaci\xc3\xb3n Martorell resulted in several Dorade\xc3\xb1os returning to settle the houses.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino195_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino195-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This tendency continued during the following three decades, with middle and high class projects being built.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino195_97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino195-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1957, a road was built between Dorado and Palo Seco.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino362_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino362-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>By the mid-20th century, Dorado continued producing sugar, fruits and cattle, but there was a continued decline in the presence of small and mid-sized properties.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino104_57-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino104-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, by this time Finlay Waymouth and Lee, Inc. disappeared.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino124_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino124-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Wealthy families, both local and American, continued acquiring the terrains of Dorado.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino124_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino124-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the 1950s, an economic shift lead to the virtual disappearance of the sugar industry and the terrains were repurposed for cattle.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino125_59-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino125-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Clara Livingston sold her terrains to the Rickefelker family, who turned them into two hotels, Dorado Beach and Cerromar.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino123_79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino123-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Late in the decade, Dorado created a Municipal Library, located in the old emergency building.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino188_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino188-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Stores such as La Vencedora and La Favorita were founded during this time, operating during the following decades.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino127_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino127-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1972, Alfonso L\xc3\xb3pez Chaar was elected mayor with a platform based on sports and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino308_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino308-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dorado became the first municipality to adopt the landfill system.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino320_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino320-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/2nd_G7_summit" title="2nd G7 summit">2nd G7 summit</a> was held at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dorado_Beach_Resort&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dorado Beach Resort (page does not exist)">Dorado Beach Resort</a> between June 27 and 28, 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>Casa del Rey, which after being acquired from the government by L\xc3\xb3pez had served as the house of several people and a political center, was restored by the municipality and the ICP during the second half of the 20th Century.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino190_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino190-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1985, the municipal administration began a restoration and redevelopment project named Dorado 2000 prior to the celebration of Dorado\'s 150th Anniversary.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino196_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino196-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When Alfonso L\xc3\xb3pez Chaar left the office of mayor to become Secretary of State in 1987, his successor Carlos L\xc3\xb3pez became the youngest mayor in Puerto Rico at that point at the age of 29.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino198_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino198-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1988 the Committee on the Quality of Life of a Puerto Rico recognized the municipality as first in their life quality rankings.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino313_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino313-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The municipality revalidated and was included into the entity\'s hall of fame.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino313_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino313-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1989, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hugo" title="Hurricane Hugo">Hurricane Hugo</a> made landfall in Puerto Rico and caused heavy damages to the municipality.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino363_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino363-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By the 1990s, Dorado del Mar had closed, with Cerromar and Dorado Beach continuing their function.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino332_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino332-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1991, a reforestation initiative named Dorado Siembra was implanted.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino363_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino363-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> That same year the ICP named the town\'s cultural program a "cultural model".<sup id="cite_ref-Canino363_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino363-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p><p>On January 6, 1992, floods destroyed the La Plata bridge.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino363_105-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino363-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> That same year, the administration of Dorado held concurrent Fifth Centenary and municipal anniversary events.<sup id="cite_ref-Canino363_105-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canino363-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dorado_Airport" title="Dorado Airport">Dorado Airport</a> was operated from 1942 to 1996. The airport began as a military landing strip, then became a civilian airport in the <a href="/wiki/1960s" title="1960s">1960s</a> with <a href="/wiki/Caribair" title="Caribair">Caribair</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dorado_Wings" title="Dorado Wings">Dorado Wings</a> flights operating until 1980 and 1982 respectively. The airport operated as a private landing strip throughout the <a href="/wiki/1980s" title="1980s">1980s</a> and then fell into disrepair. In 1996, the airport was rezoned as residential land and redeveloped.\n</p><p>On September 20, 2017 <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Maria" title="Hurricane Maria">Hurricane Maria</a> struck the island of Puerto Rico. In Dorado, 1,175 homes were left without a roof and 625 homes were destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>\n</p>\n<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:House_with_blue_tarp_after_Hurricane_Maria_in_Dorado,_Puerto_Rico.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/House_with_blue_tarp_after_Hurricane_Maria_in_Dorado%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg/220px-House_with_blue_tarp_after_Hurricane_Maria_in_Dorado%2C_Puerto_Rico.jpg"
